{"cryptosporidiosisnum_articles": "122", "cryptosporidiosis": [{"archive_id": "7676644", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - Ireland: salad mix, alert, recall", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=7676644", "date": "2018-01-11 16:15:36", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - IRELAND: (LIMERICK)\n***************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 10 Jan 2018\nSource: Limerick Leader [edited]\nhttp://www.limerickleader.ie/news/home/291006/cryptosporidium-outbreak-in-limerick-suburb-being-monitored-by-hse.html\n\n\nThe Department of Public Health (HSE) is investigating an outbreak of an infectious type of gastroenteritis in a Limerick city suburb. The HSE is looking into 11 reported cases of cryptosporidiosis, caused by the parasite _cryptosporidium_, in Castletroy, including 6 confirmed cases among children attending the Tall Trees Creche in Monaleen.\n\nPotentially fatal for those with compromised immune systems, the illness results in diarrhoea, vomiting, stomach cramps and fever. Human cryptosporidiosis became a notifiable disease in Ireland in 2004, which means all medical practitioners are required to report an incidence of the illness to the Department of Public Health.\n\nCryptosporidiosis is passed on through infected water sources, farm or animal exposure or through direct contact.\n\nLimerick's public water supply has been tested for the presence of the parasite but it has not been detected, the Department of Public Health confirmed.\n\nThe Tall Trees Creche has been temporarily closed following the outbreak in order to break the cycle of transmission, according to owner and manager Pamela Walsh Hennessy. \"We wanted to take every precaution so our cr\u00e8che has been stripped back to 4 walls and decontaminated. We started from scratch, replacing items that couldn't be sterilised, getting rid of items. It has been a huge undertaking but the most important thing is the health and safety of our kids,\" she said.\n\nChildren attending day care centres have an increased risk of contracting the illness. This is the 1st case of the parasite at the centre in 13 years, according to Ms Walsh Hennessy.\n\nA control team including representatives from the HSE and TUSLA was immediately convened to manage the current outbreak, according to the HSE spokesperson. \"Parents of the cr\u00e8che have been contacted directly by the Department of Public Health with relevant advice,\" said a spokesperson. The cr\u00e8che is expected to reopen this [Thu 11 Jan 2017], following its closure on [Sat 6 Jan 2017], but is awaiting confirmation.\n\n[Byline: Jess Casey]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection caused by _Cryptosporidium_ spp. (often _C. parvum_ in human infections) is a zoonosis with reservoir in ruminants. It is often a problem in areas where the drinking water is drawn from surface water as lakes and rivers and are resistant to chlorination. It is mentioned here that analysis of drinking water so far has been negative, but the cyst number is often low and concentration methods are needed. - Mod.EP\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nLimerick, Limerick, Ireland: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/2218.]\nSee Also\n2007\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Ireland: background: 20070413.1236\nCryptosporidiosis - Ireland (Galway) 20070409.1194\n----\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - Ireland 20020508.4131\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Northern Ireland) (02) 20010504.0862\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Northern Ireland) 20010421.0780\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Northern Ireland) (02) 20000904.1508\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Northern Ireland) 20000524.0823\n.................................................sb/ep/ao/ml", "reports": [{"diseases": ["cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2018-01-10 00:00:00", "2004-03-15 00:00:00", "2017-01-11 00:00:00", "2007-03-15 00:00:00", "2002-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-03-15 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Northern Ireland", "country": "Ireland"}, {"location": "Ireland", "country": "Ireland"}, {"location": "Limerick", "country": "Ireland"}, {"location": "Galway", "country": "Ireland"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "6841370", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - Sweden (04)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6841370", "date": "2018-01-11 16:15:36", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - IRELAND: (LIMERICK)\n***************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 10 Jan 2018\nSource: Limerick Leader [edited]\nhttp://www.limerickleader.ie/news/home/291006/cryptosporidium-outbreak-in-limerick-suburb-being-monitored-by-hse.html\n\n\nThe Department of Public Health (HSE) is investigating an outbreak of an infectious type of gastroenteritis in a Limerick city suburb. The HSE is looking into 11 reported cases of cryptosporidiosis, caused by the parasite _cryptosporidium_, in Castletroy, including 6 confirmed cases among children attending the Tall Trees Creche in Monaleen.\n\nPotentially fatal for those with compromised immune systems, the illness results in diarrhoea, vomiting, stomach cramps and fever. Human cryptosporidiosis became a notifiable disease in Ireland in 2004, which means all medical practitioners are required to report an incidence of the illness to the Department of Public Health.\n\nCryptosporidiosis is passed on through infected water sources, farm or animal exposure or through direct contact.\n\nLimerick's public water supply has been tested for the presence of the parasite but it has not been detected, the Department of Public Health confirmed.\n\nThe Tall Trees Creche has been temporarily closed following the outbreak in order to break the cycle of transmission, according to owner and manager Pamela Walsh Hennessy. \"We wanted to take every precaution so our cr\u00e8che has been stripped back to 4 walls and decontaminated. We started from scratch, replacing items that couldn't be sterilised, getting rid of items. It has been a huge undertaking but the most important thing is the health and safety of our kids,\" she said.\n\nChildren attending day care centres have an increased risk of contracting the illness. This is the 1st case of the parasite at the centre in 13 years, according to Ms Walsh Hennessy.\n\nA control team including representatives from the HSE and TUSLA was immediately convened to manage the current outbreak, according to the HSE spokesperson. \"Parents of the cr\u00e8che have been contacted directly by the Department of Public Health with relevant advice,\" said a spokesperson. The cr\u00e8che is expected to reopen this [Thu 11 Jan 2017], following its closure on [Sat 6 Jan 2017], but is awaiting confirmation.\n\n[Byline: Jess Casey]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection caused by _Cryptosporidium_ spp. (often _C. parvum_ in human infections) is a zoonosis with reservoir in ruminants. It is mentioned here that analysis of drinking water so far has been negative, but the cyst number is often low and concentration methods are needed. - Mod.EP\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nLimerick, Limerick, Ireland: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/2218.]\nSee Also\n2007\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Health officials reported 1738 cases\nacross 20 counties in western and central New York, up from 746 reported\nillnesses Thursday. Seneca Lake Park's popular Sprayground has been closed\nfor the remainder of the summer as a result.\nSo far, 13 cases in 4 different counties have been confirmed as\ncryptosporidiosis, a common waterborne disease. That's an increase from 5\nconfirmed cases Thursday. Crypto, as it is known, is very contagious and\ncan cause symptoms including diarrhea, nausea, vomiting and fever that can\nlast for weeks.\nThe Health Department initially shut down the Sprayground temporarily\nTuesday after receiving more than 100 reported cases of illness dating back\nto early July 2005. Once the public was notified of the outbreak, the\nnumber of reported illnesses then jumped to more than 700 overnight.\nTests conducted by the Health Department have found the presence of\ncryptosporidium in 2 storage tanks that supply water for the spray park,\nofficials said Friday. Additional water samples from the Seneca Lake State\nPark beach showed no health concerns with the water quality, and the beach\nremains open.\nTo prevent the further spread of illness, the Health Department advises the\nthorough washing of hands after using the toilet, changing diapers or\ncoming in contact with fecal matter in any way. Swimmers should also avoid\nswallowing water, especially in ponds, lakes and rivers, officials said.\nAdditionally, people experiencing gastrointestinal disease and who work in\nfood services, or with children or the sick should stay home from work\nuntil they have recovered. Sick children should be kept home from daycare.\nThe disease usually goes away without treatment in healthy individuals.\n[Byline: By Jessica M. Health officials reported 1738 cases\nacross 20 counties in western and central New York, up from 746 reported\nillnesses Thursday. Seneca Lake Park's popular Sprayground has been closed\nfor the remainder of the summer as a result.\nSo far, 13 cases in 4 different counties have been confirmed as\ncryptosporidiosis, a common waterborne disease. That's an increase from 5\nconfirmed cases Thursday. Crypto, as it is known, is very contagious and\ncan cause symptoms including diarrhea, nausea, vomiting and fever that can\nlast for weeks.\nThe Health Department initially shut down the Sprayground temporarily\nTuesday after receiving more than 100 reported cases of illness dating back\nto early July 2005. Once the public was notified of the outbreak, the\nnumber of reported illnesses then jumped to more than 700 overnight.\nTests conducted by the Health Department have found the presence of\ncryptosporidium in 2 storage tanks that supply water for the spray park,\nofficials said Friday. Additional water samples from the Seneca Lake State\nPark beach showed no health concerns with the water quality, and the beach\nremains open.\nTo prevent the further spread of illness, the Health Department advises the\nthorough washing of hands after using the toilet, changing diapers or\ncoming in contact with fecal matter in any way. Swimmers should also avoid\nswallowing water, especially in ponds, lakes and rivers, officials said.\nAdditionally, people experiencing gastrointestinal disease and who work in\nfood services, or with children or the sick should stay home from work\nuntil they have recovered. Sick children should be kept home from daycare.\nThe disease usually goes away without treatment in healthy individuals.\n[Byline: By Jessica M. USA (NY)(02)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2208500", "date": "2005-08-21 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, WATER PARK - USA (NEW YORK) (02)\n*************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nSponsored in part by Elsevier, publisher of\nTravel Medicine and Infectious Disease\n<http://www.travelmedicine.elsevier.com>\nDate: Sun, 21 Aug 2005\nFrom: ProMED <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: AP via Newsday [edited]\n<http://www.newsday.com/news/local/wire/ny-bc-ny--spraygroundillnes0819aug19,0,4728121.story?coll=ny-top-headlines&track=mostemailedlink>\n\nThe number of [cases of] reported gastrointestinal illnesses possibly\nspread by a state-run waterpark has now grown to nearly 2000, according to\nthe State Health Department on Friday. Health officials reported 1738 cases\nacross 20 counties in western and central New York, up from 746 reported\nillnesses Thursday. Seneca Lake Park's popular Sprayground has been closed\nfor the remainder of the summer as a result.\nSo far, 13 cases in 4 different counties have been confirmed as\ncryptosporidiosis, a common waterborne disease. That's an increase from 5\nconfirmed cases Thursday. Crypto, as it is known, is very contagious and\ncan cause symptoms including diarrhea, nausea, vomiting and fever that can\nlast for weeks.\nThe Health Department initially shut down the Sprayground temporarily\nTuesday after receiving more than 100 reported cases of illness dating back\nto early July 2005. Once the public was notified of the outbreak, the\nnumber of reported illnesses then jumped to more than 700 overnight.\nTests conducted by the Health Department have found the presence of\ncryptosporidium in 2 storage tanks that supply water for the spray park,\nofficials said Friday. Additional water samples from the Seneca Lake State\nPark beach showed no health concerns with the water quality, and the beach\nremains open.\nTo prevent the further spread of illness, the Health Department advises the\nthorough washing of hands after using the toilet, changing diapers or\ncoming in contact with fecal matter in any way. Swimmers should also avoid\nswallowing water, especially in ponds, lakes and rivers, officials said.\nAdditionally, people experiencing gastrointestinal disease and who work in\nfood services, or with children or the sick should stay home from work\nuntil they have recovered. Sick children should be kept home from daycare.\nThe disease usually goes away without treatment in healthy individuals.\n[Byline: By Jessica M. Pasko, AP]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, water park - USA (NY) 20050819.2442\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - USA (MT) 20050818.2415\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - Australia (NSW) 20050617.1710\n2003\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - USA (Kansas) 20030825.2148\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (02) 20030806.1935\nCryptosporidiosis - Spain: background 20030802.1894\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Illinois) 20010818.1957\nDiarrhea, recreational water - USA 20010528.1035\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, pool-associated - USA (Ohio) 20000805.1307\nCryptosporidiosis, pool-associated - USA (Ohio) (02) 20000808.1321\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Florida) 20000905.1513\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Ohio) 20000825.1420\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Ohio) (02) 20000904.1505\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidium, false positive ELISA tests - USA 19990116.0065\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Oregon) 19981001.1950\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Texas) 19980730.1442\nCryptosporidiosis - USA (Texas) (03) 19980808.1551\n...............................ep/pg/dk\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. USA (NY)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2208491", "date": "2005-04-24 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - UK (SCOTLAND)\n*****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 24 Apr 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The Scotsman [edited]\n<http://news.scotsman.com/health.cfm?id=432622005>\n\n\nFears that 200 may be affected after wildlife visit\n---------------------------------------------------\nA popular wildlife park at the center of a health scare remained open to\nthe public yesterday [23 Apr 2005], as the number of adults and children\naffected by a stomach bug continued to escalate, with fears that more than\n200 people may have been infected.\n\n6 children were admitted to the hospital earlier this week [4th week April\n2005] after being infected by the cryptosporidium bug following a visit to\nthe Auchingarrich Wildlife Centre, near Comrie, in Perthshire.\n\n2 days ago, NHS Tayside revealed that the health authority's outbreak\ncontrol team had been notified of 13 confirmed and 18 suspected cases of\ninfection by the contagious parasite, the symptoms of which include stomach\nupsets, diarrhea and fever. But yesterday [23 Apr 2005], the number of\nconfirmed cases increased to 32, with 200 suspected cases also reported to\nthe health authority.\n\nThe children and adults are believed to have become infected with the bug\nwhile petting and hand-feeding newborn lambs in a barn at the center. The\nlambs have already been removed from the park as a precautionary measure. A\nspokesman for NHS Tayside said yesterday [23 Apr 2005]: \"There are now 32\nconfirmed cases, although we expect that number to continue to rise. A\nfurther 200 people who visited Auchingarrich have reported becoming ill\nwith symptoms consistent with cryptosporidium infection. It is very\nimportant to stress, however, that these are not confirmed cases, and\nfurther investigations are ongoing, and it is unlikely that all will have\nbeen infected. Laboratory tests are continuing, and we hope to give a\nfurther update early next week.\" He added: \"There is only one child still\nin hospital, but no one is seriously ill.\" The spokesman said the center\nwould remain open. He explained: \"The view of the incident control team is\nthat the center has voluntarily suspended animal petting, and the facility\nwhere that happened has been closed. The bales of hay within it have been\nburned, and it has been disinfected. Our view at the moment is that the\nrisk to the public has been removed, although we have to remind people\nagain and again to please wash their hands after they have been out in a\nrural setting and have been in contact with animals or their environment.\nIt is absolutely vital to observe good hygiene.\"\n\nCryptosporidium is a parasite which is commonly found in animal intestines.\nIt can cause diarrhea, vomiting, stomach cramps and a slight fever. Members\nof the outbreak control team from NHS Tayside and officials from Perth and\nKinross Council's environmental health service are continuing an\ninvestigation into the outbreak. Scotland's largest cryptosporidium\noutbreak of recent times was in Glasgow in 1998 when more than 300 people\nwere confirmed as having been affected by the stomach bug.\n\n[byline: Frank Urquhart]\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[ProMED-mail reported in October 2003 that more than 200 cases had been\nreported from Scotland since March that year. As stressed in this message,\nnot all cases with gastrointestinal symptoms have been diagnosed with\ncryptosporidium infection.\n\nCryptosporidium is typically an intestinal parasite of calves, not lambs,\nand contact with animals may not be a particular risk factor, except when\nthe animal has diarrhea at the time of contact. Other infections may also\nbe possible, like Vero-toxin-producing _Escherichia coli_ (VTEC), for which\ncontact with animals is a well recognized risk factor. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland): background 20031015.2595\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20031014.2591\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (02) 20030806.1935\nCryptosporidiosis - Spain: background 20030802.1894\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2000\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\n1999\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\n1998\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520.0963\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510.0927\n\n..........................ep/msp/sh\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. USA (MO)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2208472", "date": "2005-04-24 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - UK (SCOTLAND)\n*****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 24 Apr 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The Scotsman [edited]\n<http://news.scotsman.com/health.cfm?id=432622005>\n\n\nFears that 200 may be affected after wildlife visit\n---------------------------------------------------\nA popular wildlife park at the center of a health scare remained open to\nthe public yesterday [23 Apr 2005], as the number of adults and children\naffected by a stomach bug continued to escalate, with fears that more than\n200 people may have been infected.\n\n6 children were admitted to the hospital earlier this week [4th week April\n2005] after being infected by the cryptosporidium bug following a visit to\nthe Auchingarrich Wildlife Centre, near Comrie, in Perthshire.\n\n2 days ago, NHS Tayside revealed that the health authority's outbreak\ncontrol team had been notified of 13 confirmed and 18 suspected cases of\ninfection by the contagious parasite, the symptoms of which include stomach\nupsets, diarrhea and fever. But yesterday [23 Apr 2005], the number of\nconfirmed cases increased to 32, with 200 suspected cases also reported to\nthe health authority.\n\nThe children and adults are believed to have become infected with the bug\nwhile petting and hand-feeding newborn lambs in a barn at the center. The\nlambs have already been removed from the park as a precautionary measure. A\nspokesman for NHS Tayside said yesterday [23 Apr 2005]: \"There are now 32\nconfirmed cases, although we expect that number to continue to rise. A\nfurther 200 people who visited Auchingarrich have reported becoming ill\nwith symptoms consistent with cryptosporidium infection. It is very\nimportant to stress, however, that these are not confirmed cases, and\nfurther investigations are ongoing, and it is unlikely that all will have\nbeen infected. Laboratory tests are continuing, and we hope to give a\nfurther update early next week.\" He added: \"There is only one child still\nin hospital, but no one is seriously ill.\" The spokesman said the center\nwould remain open. He explained: \"The view of the incident control team is\nthat the center has voluntarily suspended animal petting, and the facility\nwhere that happened has been closed. The bales of hay within it have been\nburned, and it has been disinfected. Our view at the moment is that the\nrisk to the public has been removed, although we have to remind people\nagain and again to please wash their hands after they have been out in a\nrural setting and have been in contact with animals or their environment.\nIt is absolutely vital to observe good hygiene.\"\n\nCryptosporidium is a parasite which is commonly found in animal intestines.\nIt can cause diarrhea, vomiting, stomach cramps and a slight fever. Members\nof the outbreak control team from NHS Tayside and officials from Perth and\nKinross Council's environmental health service are continuing an\ninvestigation into the outbreak. Scotland's largest cryptosporidium\noutbreak of recent times was in Glasgow in 1998 when more than 300 people\nwere confirmed as having been affected by the stomach bug.\n\n[byline: Frank Urquhart]\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[ProMED-mail reported in October 2003 that more than 200 cases had been\nreported from Scotland since March that year. As stressed in this message,\nnot all cases with gastrointestinal symptoms have been diagnosed with\ncryptosporidium infection.\n\nCryptosporidium is typically an intestinal parasite of calves, not lambs,\nand contact with animals may not be a particular risk factor, except when\nthe animal has diarrhea at the time of contact. Other infections may also\nbe possible, like Vero-toxin-producing _Escherichia coli_ (VTEC), for which\ncontact with animals is a well recognized risk factor. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland): background 20031015.2595\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20031014.2591\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (02) 20030806.1935\nCryptosporidiosis - Spain: background 20030802.1894\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2000\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\n1999\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\n1998\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520.0963\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510.0927\n\n..........................ep/msp/sh\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. UK (Scotland)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2207751", "date": "2005-04-24 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - UK (SCOTLAND)\n*****************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: 24 Apr 2005\nFrom: ProMED-mail <promed@promedmail.org>\nSource: The Scotsman [edited]\n<http://news.scotsman.com/health.cfm?id=432622005>\n\n\nFears that 200 may be affected after wildlife visit\n---------------------------------------------------\nA popular wildlife park at the center of a health scare remained open to\nthe public yesterday [23 Apr 2005], as the number of adults and children\naffected by a stomach bug continued to escalate, with fears that more than\n200 people may have been infected.\n\n6 children were admitted to the hospital earlier this week [4th week April\n2005] after being infected by the cryptosporidium bug following a visit to\nthe Auchingarrich Wildlife Centre, near Comrie, in Perthshire.\n\n2 days ago, NHS Tayside revealed that the health authority's outbreak\ncontrol team had been notified of 13 confirmed and 18 suspected cases of\ninfection by the contagious parasite, the symptoms of which include stomach\nupsets, diarrhea and fever. But yesterday [23 Apr 2005], the number of\nconfirmed cases increased to 32, with 200 suspected cases also reported to\nthe health authority.\n\nThe children and adults are believed to have become infected with the bug\nwhile petting and hand-feeding newborn lambs in a barn at the center. The\nlambs have already been removed from the park as a precautionary measure. A\nspokesman for NHS Tayside said yesterday [23 Apr 2005]: \"There are now 32\nconfirmed cases, although we expect that number to continue to rise. A\nfurther 200 people who visited Auchingarrich have reported becoming ill\nwith symptoms consistent with cryptosporidium infection. It is very\nimportant to stress, however, that these are not confirmed cases, and\nfurther investigations are ongoing, and it is unlikely that all will have\nbeen infected. Laboratory tests are continuing, and we hope to give a\nfurther update early next week.\" He added: \"There is only one child still\nin hospital, but no one is seriously ill.\" The spokesman said the center\nwould remain open. He explained: \"The view of the incident control team is\nthat the center has voluntarily suspended animal petting, and the facility\nwhere that happened has been closed. The bales of hay within it have been\nburned, and it has been disinfected. Our view at the moment is that the\nrisk to the public has been removed, although we have to remind people\nagain and again to please wash their hands after they have been out in a\nrural setting and have been in contact with animals or their environment.\nIt is absolutely vital to observe good hygiene.\"\n\nCryptosporidium is a parasite which is commonly found in animal intestines.\nIt can cause diarrhea, vomiting, stomach cramps and a slight fever. Members\nof the outbreak control team from NHS Tayside and officials from Perth and\nKinross Council's environmental health service are continuing an\ninvestigation into the outbreak. Scotland's largest cryptosporidium\noutbreak of recent times was in Glasgow in 1998 when more than 300 people\nwere confirmed as having been affected by the stomach bug.\n\n[byline: Frank Urquhart]\n\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[ProMED-mail reported in October 2003 that more than 200 cases had been\nreported from Scotland since March that year. As stressed in this message,\nnot all cases with gastrointestinal symptoms have been diagnosed with\ncryptosporidium infection.\n\nCryptosporidium is typically an intestinal parasite of calves, not lambs,\nand contact with animals may not be a particular risk factor, except when\nthe animal has diarrhea at the time of contact. Other infections may also\nbe possible, like Vero-toxin-producing _Escherichia coli_ (VTEC), for which\ncontact with animals is a well recognized risk factor. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\n2003\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland): background 20031015.2595\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20031014.2591\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (02) 20030806.1935\nCryptosporidiosis - Spain: background 20030802.1894\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2000\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\n1999\n---\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\n1998\n---\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520.0963\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510.0927\n\n..........................ep/msp/sh\n\n\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-promed@promedmail.org.\n\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["1998-03-15 00:00:00", "2003-10-15 00:00:00", "2003-03-15 00:00:00", "2002-03-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "1998-03-15 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "Perthshire", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "Comrie", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "Perth", "country": "Australia"}, {"location": "England", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204731", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland): background", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204731", "date": "2003-08-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (02)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 6 Aug 2003\nFrom: Rodney Cartwright <cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n\nUpdate on the number of British tourists with Cryptosporidia infection\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe number of confirmed and reported cases in British tourists, mainly\nchildren, returning from Alcudia is 188. The majority are associated with\none hotel, but there are a few cases associated with other hotels and with\nno history of having visited the main outbreak hotel. 2 possibilities that\nneed to be considered are contamination of the drinking water supply or\ncontamination of a water theme park used as a popular excursion venue.\nReports are being received of a small number of cases associated with other\nresorts in Mallorca and Menorca.\n\n[Prof. Cartwright has provided information on management of swimming pools\nprovided below. - Mod.MPP]\nThe management of swimming pools associated with Cryptosporidiosis or\ncontaminated by Cryptosporidium oocysts; Pilot Guidance for the U.K.\nFederation of Tour Operators Prepared by Prof. Rodney Cartwright August 2003\nThese notes have been prepared for guidance on the actions to be taken when\ncryptosporidium infection is associated with a swimming pool. They do not\ncover action for prevention of contamination such as a faecal accident\npolicy. Many of the procedures recommended should be undertaken only by an\noperative with the appropriate training. The use of high chlorine levels\nis hazardous and the appropriate health and safety precautions must be\nfollowed.\n1. I am unaware of any published procedure for the decontamination of a\nswimming pool contaminated with cryptosporidium oocysts. There are no\npublished studies on the efficacy of any procedures on the disinfection of\nswimming pools against cryptosporidium, although there is anecdotal\nevidence that certain actions have been associated with the cessation of\nfurther cases of cryptosporidiosis.\n2. There are recommendations for a response to a faecal accident. US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention in their weekly publication\n(MMWR) of 25 May 2001 includes advice for contamination by a liquid stool.\na. Raise the free available chlorine concentration to 20 mg/L and maintain\nthe pH between 7.2 and 7.5. Ensure this concentration is found throughout\nall co-circulating pools by sampling at least 3 widely spaced locations\naway from return water outlets. This chlorine and pH level should be\nsufficient to inactivate Cryptosporidium and should be maintained for at\nleast 8 hours, equivalent to a CT inactivation value of 9600. A higher or\nlower free available chlorine level/inactivation time can be used as long\nas a CT inactivation value equalling 9600 is maintained for Cryptosporidium\ninactivation. State or local regulators may require higher free available\nchlorine levels in the presence of chlorine stabilizers such as chlorinated\nisocyanurates. If necessary, consult an aquatics professional to determine\nand identify the feasibility, practical methods, and safety considerations\nbefore attempting the hyperchlorination of any pool.\nb. Ensure that the filtration system is operating while the pool reaches\nand maintains the proper free available chlorine concentration during\ndisinfection.\nc. Backwash the filter thoroughly after reaching the CT value. Be sure the\neffluent is discharged directly to waste and in accordance with state or\nlocal regulations. Do not return the backwash through the filter. Where\nappropriate, replace the filter media.\nd. Swimmers may be allowed into the pool after the required CT value has\nbeen achieved and the free available chlorine level has been returned to\nthe normal operating range allowed by the state or local regulatory\nauthority. Maintain the free available chlorine concentration and pH at\nstandard operating levels based on state or local regulations. If\nnecessary, consult state or local regulatory authorities for\nrecommendations on bringing the free available chlorine levels back to an\nacceptable operating range.\nThis guidance assumes a correctly installed and maintained water treatment\nsystem.\n3. The UK Pool Water Treatment Advisory Group (PWTAG) Swimming Pool Water\n-treatment and Quality Standards (1999) following a faecal accident where\nthere is a possibility of cryptosporidium contamination it states and\nrecommends that:\nThe appropriate procedure relies on the fact that the cysts are, in\nprinciple, large enough to be filtered out.\n- The pool is cleared of people immediately.\n- Disinfectant levels are maintained at the top of the recommended range.\n- The pool is vacuumed and swept.\n- Using a coagulant, the water is filtered for 6 turnover cycles (which\ncould well take up to a day, and so might mean closing the pool until the\nnext day).\n- The filter is backwashed.\n- The pool can then be reopened.\nClearly this is a fairly drastic course of action that any pool operator\nwould want to avoid if possible. But if there is a good reason to suspect\nthat Cryptosporidium or Giardia is responsible (certainly if the person\ninvolved has had diarrhoea for some days), it would be the safest\nprocedure. The local consultant in communicable disease control (CCDC)\nshould be notified. A copy of such emergency measures can usefully be\nincluded in the operation and maintenance manual.\n4. In the PWTAG technical guidance note of February 2001 on Cryptosporidium\nconcentrates on coagulation and filtration but does not deal directly with\ndecontamination of a known contaminated pool. They mention chlorine at a\nlevel of 30mg/L for 4 hours as being necessary to kill cryptosporidium\noocysts but make no recommendations on the use of chlorine. Further\ndetailed advice is given on the use of coagulants and filters.\n5. In the UK there is concern on relying on high chlorine levels alone as a\nmeans of disinfection because\na. There are no published data on the effect of chlorine on cryptosporidium\noocysts in a swimming pool. It is understood that the studies that have\nbeen undertaken are in a laboratory setting.\nb. Chlorine is inactivated by organic material and there may be high levels\nof such material in parts of a pool and especially in filters.\nc. It would be necessary for the chlorine to be evenly distributed\nthroughout the pool.\nd. For chlorine to be effective the pH must be carefully controlled (CDC\nrecommend 7.2 - 7.5)\ne. Other chemicals may interfere with the action of the chlorine.\n6. Concerns with filtration include\na. The need for the filters to be operated at the correct pressure and\nwater flow. The optimum operating conditions should be provided by the\nfilter suppliers\nb. The use of sand of the correct particle size in the filters.\nc. Proper maintenance and back washing of the filters.\nd. The time taken for all the pool water to be filtered. Water equivalent\nto the pool volume may pass through in a given time, but the structure of\nthe pool may be such that all the water has not passed through. That is\nthere may be areas of poor circulation in the pool.\n7. Water can be tested for the presence of cryptosporidium\noocysts. Normally 10L grab samples are tested but larger volumes can be\nexamined by the use of a cartridge filter. The volume of water and\ncollection method should be discussed with the test laboratory. Before\nconsidering the appropriate action, an understanding of the water test\nresults is necessary so that misunderstandings do not occur.\na. Cryptosporidium oocysts are not evenly distributed in the\nwater. Therefore the numbers in a sample do not on their own have any\nsignificance. If oocysts are present, that water is contaminated and poses\na potential risk to bathers.\nb. The absence of oocysts in a sample does not mean that there are no\noocysts elsewhere in the pool that could be a hazard to bathers.\nc. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 \"Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA\"\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 \"Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA\"\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-majordomo@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2003-08-15 00:00:00", "2001-05-25 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-02-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-03-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "1998-03-15 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "ph", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Victoria", "country": "Seychelles"}, {"location": "pH", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "New Zealand", "country": "New Zealand"}, {"location": "U.K.", "country": "Mongolia"}, {"location": "England", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204605", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - USA (KS) (04)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204605", "date": "2003-08-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (02)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 6 Aug 2003\nFrom: Rodney Cartwright <cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n\nUpdate on the number of British tourists with Cryptosporidia infection\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe number of confirmed and reported cases in British tourists, mainly\nchildren, returning from Alcudia is 188. The majority are associated with\none hotel, but there are a few cases associated with other hotels and with\nno history of having visited the main outbreak hotel. 2 possibilities that\nneed to be considered are contamination of the drinking water supply or\ncontamination of a water theme park used as a popular excursion venue.\nReports are being received of a small number of cases associated with other\nresorts in Mallorca and Menorca.\n\n[Prof. Cartwright has provided information on management of swimming pools\nprovided below. - Mod.MPP]\nThe management of swimming pools associated with Cryptosporidiosis or\ncontaminated by Cryptosporidium oocysts; Pilot Guidance for the U.K.\nFederation of Tour Operators Prepared by Prof. Rodney Cartwright August 2003\nThese notes have been prepared for guidance on the actions to be taken when\ncryptosporidium infection is associated with a swimming pool. They do not\ncover action for prevention of contamination such as a faecal accident\npolicy. Many of the procedures recommended should be undertaken only by an\noperative with the appropriate training. The use of high chlorine levels\nis hazardous and the appropriate health and safety precautions must be\nfollowed.\n1. I am unaware of any published procedure for the decontamination of a\nswimming pool contaminated with cryptosporidium oocysts. There are no\npublished studies on the efficacy of any procedures on the disinfection of\nswimming pools against cryptosporidium, although there is anecdotal\nevidence that certain actions have been associated with the cessation of\nfurther cases of cryptosporidiosis.\n2. There are recommendations for a response to a faecal accident. US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention in their weekly publication\n(MMWR) of 25 May 2001 includes advice for contamination by a liquid stool.\na. Raise the free available chlorine concentration to 20 mg/L and maintain\nthe pH between 7.2 and 7.5. Ensure this concentration is found throughout\nall co-circulating pools by sampling at least 3 widely spaced locations\naway from return water outlets. This chlorine and pH level should be\nsufficient to inactivate Cryptosporidium and should be maintained for at\nleast 8 hours, equivalent to a CT inactivation value of 9600. A higher or\nlower free available chlorine level/inactivation time can be used as long\nas a CT inactivation value equalling 9600 is maintained for Cryptosporidium\ninactivation. State or local regulators may require higher free available\nchlorine levels in the presence of chlorine stabilizers such as chlorinated\nisocyanurates. If necessary, consult an aquatics professional to determine\nand identify the feasibility, practical methods, and safety considerations\nbefore attempting the hyperchlorination of any pool.\nb. Ensure that the filtration system is operating while the pool reaches\nand maintains the proper free available chlorine concentration during\ndisinfection.\nc. Backwash the filter thoroughly after reaching the CT value. Be sure the\neffluent is discharged directly to waste and in accordance with state or\nlocal regulations. Do not return the backwash through the filter. Where\nappropriate, replace the filter media.\nd. Swimmers may be allowed into the pool after the required CT value has\nbeen achieved and the free available chlorine level has been returned to\nthe normal operating range allowed by the state or local regulatory\nauthority. Maintain the free available chlorine concentration and pH at\nstandard operating levels based on state or local regulations. If\nnecessary, consult state or local regulatory authorities for\nrecommendations on bringing the free available chlorine levels back to an\nacceptable operating range.\nThis guidance assumes a correctly installed and maintained water treatment\nsystem.\n3. The UK Pool Water Treatment Advisory Group (PWTAG) Swimming Pool Water\n-treatment and Quality Standards (1999) following a faecal accident where\nthere is a possibility of cryptosporidium contamination it states and\nrecommends that:\nThe appropriate procedure relies on the fact that the cysts are, in\nprinciple, large enough to be filtered out.\n- The pool is cleared of people immediately.\n- Disinfectant levels are maintained at the top of the recommended range.\n- The pool is vacuumed and swept.\n- Using a coagulant, the water is filtered for 6 turnover cycles (which\ncould well take up to a day, and so might mean closing the pool until the\nnext day).\n- The filter is backwashed.\n- The pool can then be reopened.\nClearly this is a fairly drastic course of action that any pool operator\nwould want to avoid if possible. But if there is a good reason to suspect\nthat Cryptosporidium or Giardia is responsible (certainly if the person\ninvolved has had diarrhoea for some days), it would be the safest\nprocedure. The local consultant in communicable disease control (CCDC)\nshould be notified. A copy of such emergency measures can usefully be\nincluded in the operation and maintenance manual.\n4. In the PWTAG technical guidance note of February 2001 on Cryptosporidium\nconcentrates on coagulation and filtration but does not deal directly with\ndecontamination of a known contaminated pool. They mention chlorine at a\nlevel of 30mg/L for 4 hours as being necessary to kill cryptosporidium\noocysts but make no recommendations on the use of chlorine. Further\ndetailed advice is given on the use of coagulants and filters.\n5. In the UK there is concern on relying on high chlorine levels alone as a\nmeans of disinfection because\na. There are no published data on the effect of chlorine on cryptosporidium\noocysts in a swimming pool. It is understood that the studies that have\nbeen undertaken are in a laboratory setting.\nb. Chlorine is inactivated by organic material and there may be high levels\nof such material in parts of a pool and especially in filters.\nc. It would be necessary for the chlorine to be evenly distributed\nthroughout the pool.\nd. For chlorine to be effective the pH must be carefully controlled (CDC\nrecommend 7.2 - 7.5)\ne. Other chemicals may interfere with the action of the chlorine.\n6. Concerns with filtration include\na. The need for the filters to be operated at the correct pressure and\nwater flow. The optimum operating conditions should be provided by the\nfilter suppliers\nb. The use of sand of the correct particle size in the filters.\nc. Proper maintenance and back washing of the filters.\nd. The time taken for all the pool water to be filtered. Water equivalent\nto the pool volume may pass through in a given time, but the structure of\nthe pool may be such that all the water has not passed through. That is\nthere may be areas of poor circulation in the pool.\n7. Water can be tested for the presence of cryptosporidium\noocysts. Normally 10L grab samples are tested but larger volumes can be\nexamined by the use of a cartridge filter. The volume of water and\ncollection method should be discussed with the test laboratory. Before\nconsidering the appropriate action, an understanding of the water test\nresults is necessary so that misunderstandings do not occur.\na. Cryptosporidium oocysts are not evenly distributed in the\nwater. Therefore the numbers in a sample do not on their own have any\nsignificance. If oocysts are present, that water is contaminated and poses\na potential risk to bathers.\nb. The absence of oocysts in a sample does not mean that there are no\noocysts elsewhere in the pool that could be a hazard to bathers.\nc. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 \"Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA\"\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
