For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-majordomo@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2003-08-15 00:00:00", "2001-05-25 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-02-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-03-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "1998-03-15 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "ph", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Victoria", "country": "Seychelles"}, {"location": "pH", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "New Zealand", "country": "New Zealand"}, {"location": "U.K.", "country": "Mongolia"}, {"location": "England", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204478", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - USA (Kansas)(02)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204478", "date": "2003-08-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (02)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 6 Aug 2003\nFrom: Rodney Cartwright <cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n\nUpdate on the number of British tourists with Cryptosporidia infection\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe number of confirmed and reported cases in British tourists, mainly\nchildren, returning from Alcudia is 188. The majority are associated with\none hotel, but there are a few cases associated with other hotels and with\nno history of having visited the main outbreak hotel. 2 possibilities that\nneed to be considered are contamination of the drinking water supply or\ncontamination of a water theme park used as a popular excursion venue.\nReports are being received of a small number of cases associated with other\nresorts in Mallorca and Menorca.\n\n[Prof. Cartwright has provided information on management of swimming pools\nprovided below. - Mod.MPP]\nThe management of swimming pools associated with Cryptosporidiosis or\ncontaminated by Cryptosporidium oocysts; Pilot Guidance for the U.K.\nFederation of Tour Operators Prepared by Prof. Rodney Cartwright August 2003\nThese notes have been prepared for guidance on the actions to be taken when\ncryptosporidium infection is associated with a swimming pool. They do not\ncover action for prevention of contamination such as a faecal accident\npolicy. Many of the procedures recommended should be undertaken only by an\noperative with the appropriate training. The use of high chlorine levels\nis hazardous and the appropriate health and safety precautions must be\nfollowed.\n1. I am unaware of any published procedure for the decontamination of a\nswimming pool contaminated with cryptosporidium oocysts. There are no\npublished studies on the efficacy of any procedures on the disinfection of\nswimming pools against cryptosporidium, although there is anecdotal\nevidence that certain actions have been associated with the cessation of\nfurther cases of cryptosporidiosis.\n2. There are recommendations for a response to a faecal accident. US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention in their weekly publication\n(MMWR) of 25 May 2001 includes advice for contamination by a liquid stool.\na. Raise the free available chlorine concentration to 20 mg/L and maintain\nthe pH between 7.2 and 7.5. Ensure this concentration is found throughout\nall co-circulating pools by sampling at least 3 widely spaced locations\naway from return water outlets. This chlorine and pH level should be\nsufficient to inactivate Cryptosporidium and should be maintained for at\nleast 8 hours, equivalent to a CT inactivation value of 9600. A higher or\nlower free available chlorine level/inactivation time can be used as long\nas a CT inactivation value equalling 9600 is maintained for Cryptosporidium\ninactivation. State or local regulators may require higher free available\nchlorine levels in the presence of chlorine stabilizers such as chlorinated\nisocyanurates. If necessary, consult an aquatics professional to determine\nand identify the feasibility, practical methods, and safety considerations\nbefore attempting the hyperchlorination of any pool.\nb. Ensure that the filtration system is operating while the pool reaches\nand maintains the proper free available chlorine concentration during\ndisinfection.\nc. Backwash the filter thoroughly after reaching the CT value. Be sure the\neffluent is discharged directly to waste and in accordance with state or\nlocal regulations. Do not return the backwash through the filter. Where\nappropriate, replace the filter media.\nd. Swimmers may be allowed into the pool after the required CT value has\nbeen achieved and the free available chlorine level has been returned to\nthe normal operating range allowed by the state or local regulatory\nauthority. Maintain the free available chlorine concentration and pH at\nstandard operating levels based on state or local regulations. If\nnecessary, consult state or local regulatory authorities for\nrecommendations on bringing the free available chlorine levels back to an\nacceptable operating range.\nThis guidance assumes a correctly installed and maintained water treatment\nsystem.\n3. The UK Pool Water Treatment Advisory Group (PWTAG) Swimming Pool Water\n-treatment and Quality Standards (1999) following a faecal accident where\nthere is a possibility of cryptosporidium contamination it states and\nrecommends that:\nThe appropriate procedure relies on the fact that the cysts are, in\nprinciple, large enough to be filtered out.\n- The pool is cleared of people immediately.\n- Disinfectant levels are maintained at the top of the recommended range.\n- The pool is vacuumed and swept.\n- Using a coagulant, the water is filtered for 6 turnover cycles (which\ncould well take up to a day, and so might mean closing the pool until the\nnext day).\n- The filter is backwashed.\n- The pool can then be reopened.\nClearly this is a fairly drastic course of action that any pool operator\nwould want to avoid if possible. But if there is a good reason to suspect\nthat Cryptosporidium or Giardia is responsible (certainly if the person\ninvolved has had diarrhoea for some days), it would be the safest\nprocedure. The local consultant in communicable disease control (CCDC)\nshould be notified. A copy of such emergency measures can usefully be\nincluded in the operation and maintenance manual.\n4. In the PWTAG technical guidance note of February 2001 on Cryptosporidium\nconcentrates on coagulation and filtration but does not deal directly with\ndecontamination of a known contaminated pool. They mention chlorine at a\nlevel of 30mg/L for 4 hours as being necessary to kill cryptosporidium\noocysts but make no recommendations on the use of chlorine. Further\ndetailed advice is given on the use of coagulants and filters.\n5. In the UK there is concern on relying on high chlorine levels alone as a\nmeans of disinfection because\na. There are no published data on the effect of chlorine on cryptosporidium\noocysts in a swimming pool. It is understood that the studies that have\nbeen undertaken are in a laboratory setting.\nb. Chlorine is inactivated by organic material and there may be high levels\nof such material in parts of a pool and especially in filters.\nc. It would be necessary for the chlorine to be evenly distributed\nthroughout the pool.\nd. For chlorine to be effective the pH must be carefully controlled (CDC\nrecommend 7.2 - 7.5)\ne. Other chemicals may interfere with the action of the chlorine.\n6. Concerns with filtration include\na. The need for the filters to be operated at the correct pressure and\nwater flow. The optimum operating conditions should be provided by the\nfilter suppliers\nb. The use of sand of the correct particle size in the filters.\nc. Proper maintenance and back washing of the filters.\nd. The time taken for all the pool water to be filtered. Water equivalent\nto the pool volume may pass through in a given time, but the structure of\nthe pool may be such that all the water has not passed through. That is\nthere may be areas of poor circulation in the pool.\n7. Water can be tested for the presence of cryptosporidium\noocysts. Normally 10L grab samples are tested but larger volumes can be\nexamined by the use of a cartridge filter. The volume of water and\ncollection method should be discussed with the test laboratory. Before\nconsidering the appropriate action, an understanding of the water test\nresults is necessary so that misunderstandings do not occur.\na. Cryptosporidium oocysts are not evenly distributed in the\nwater. Therefore the numbers in a sample do not on their own have any\nsignificance. If oocysts are present, that water is contaminated and poses\na potential risk to bathers.\nb. The absence of oocysts in a sample does not mean that there are no\noocysts elsewhere in the pool that could be a hazard to bathers.\nc. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 \"Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA\"\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. If you do not give your\nfull name and affiliation, it may not be posted. Send\ncommands to subscribe/unsubscribe, get archives, help,\netc. to: majordomo@promedmail.org. For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-majordomo@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "cryptosporidiosis"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2003-08-15 00:00:00", "2001-05-25 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-02-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "2001-03-15 00:00:00", "1999-03-15 00:00:00", "1998-03-15 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "ph", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Victoria", "country": "Seychelles"}, {"location": "pH", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "UK", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Scotland", "country": "United Kingdom"}, {"location": "New Zealand", "country": "New Zealand"}, {"location": "U.K.", "country": "Mongolia"}, {"location": "England", "country": "United States"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2204376", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Cryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) (03)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2204376", "date": "2003-08-06 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS, SWIMMING POOL - SPAIN (MAJORCA) (02)\n********************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Wed 6 Aug 2003\nFrom: Rodney Cartwright <cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n\nUpdate on the number of British tourists with Cryptosporidia infection\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe number of confirmed and reported cases in British tourists, mainly\nchildren, returning from Alcudia is 188. The majority are associated with\none hotel, but there are a few cases associated with other hotels and with\nno history of having visited the main outbreak hotel. 2 possibilities that\nneed to be considered are contamination of the drinking water supply or\ncontamination of a water theme park used as a popular excursion venue.\nReports are being received of a small number of cases associated with other\nresorts in Mallorca and Menorca.\n\n[Prof. Cartwright has provided information on management of swimming pools\nprovided below. - Mod.MPP]\nThe management of swimming pools associated with Cryptosporidiosis or\ncontaminated by Cryptosporidium oocysts; Pilot Guidance for the U.K.\nFederation of Tour Operators Prepared by Prof. Rodney Cartwright August 2003\nThese notes have been prepared for guidance on the actions to be taken when\ncryptosporidium infection is associated with a swimming pool. They do not\ncover action for prevention of contamination such as a faecal accident\npolicy. Many of the procedures recommended should be undertaken only by an\noperative with the appropriate training. The use of high chlorine levels\nis hazardous and the appropriate health and safety precautions must be\nfollowed.\n1. I am unaware of any published procedure for the decontamination of a\nswimming pool contaminated with cryptosporidium oocysts. There are no\npublished studies on the efficacy of any procedures on the disinfection of\nswimming pools against cryptosporidium, although there is anecdotal\nevidence that certain actions have been associated with the cessation of\nfurther cases of cryptosporidiosis.\n2. There are recommendations for a response to a faecal accident. US\nCenters for Disease Control and Prevention in their weekly publication\n(MMWR) of 25 May 2001 includes advice for contamination by a liquid stool.\na. Raise the free available chlorine concentration to 20 mg/L and maintain\nthe pH between 7.2 and 7.5. Ensure this concentration is found throughout\nall co-circulating pools by sampling at least 3 widely spaced locations\naway from return water outlets. This chlorine and pH level should be\nsufficient to inactivate Cryptosporidium and should be maintained for at\nleast 8 hours, equivalent to a CT inactivation value of 9600. A higher or\nlower free available chlorine level/inactivation time can be used as long\nas a CT inactivation value equalling 9600 is maintained for Cryptosporidium\ninactivation. State or local regulators may require higher free available\nchlorine levels in the presence of chlorine stabilizers such as chlorinated\nisocyanurates. If necessary, consult an aquatics professional to determine\nand identify the feasibility, practical methods, and safety considerations\nbefore attempting the hyperchlorination of any pool.\nb. Ensure that the filtration system is operating while the pool reaches\nand maintains the proper free available chlorine concentration during\ndisinfection.\nc. Backwash the filter thoroughly after reaching the CT value. Be sure the\neffluent is discharged directly to waste and in accordance with state or\nlocal regulations. Do not return the backwash through the filter. Where\nappropriate, replace the filter media.\nd. Swimmers may be allowed into the pool after the required CT value has\nbeen achieved and the free available chlorine level has been returned to\nthe normal operating range allowed by the state or local regulatory\nauthority. Maintain the free available chlorine concentration and pH at\nstandard operating levels based on state or local regulations. If\nnecessary, consult state or local regulatory authorities for\nrecommendations on bringing the free available chlorine levels back to an\nacceptable operating range.\nThis guidance assumes a correctly installed and maintained water treatment\nsystem.\n3. The UK Pool Water Treatment Advisory Group (PWTAG) Swimming Pool Water\n-treatment and Quality Standards (1999) following a faecal accident where\nthere is a possibility of cryptosporidium contamination it states and\nrecommends that:\nThe appropriate procedure relies on the fact that the cysts are, in\nprinciple, large enough to be filtered out.\n- The pool is cleared of people immediately.\n- Disinfectant levels are maintained at the top of the recommended range.\n- The pool is vacuumed and swept.\n- Using a coagulant, the water is filtered for 6 turnover cycles (which\ncould well take up to a day, and so might mean closing the pool until the\nnext day).\n- The filter is backwashed.\n- The pool can then be reopened.\nClearly this is a fairly drastic course of action that any pool operator\nwould want to avoid if possible. But if there is a good reason to suspect\nthat Cryptosporidium or Giardia is responsible (certainly if the person\ninvolved has had diarrhoea for some days), it would be the safest\nprocedure. The local consultant in communicable disease control (CCDC)\nshould be notified. A copy of such emergency measures can usefully be\nincluded in the operation and maintenance manual.\n4. In the PWTAG technical guidance note of February 2001 on Cryptosporidium\nconcentrates on coagulation and filtration but does not deal directly with\ndecontamination of a known contaminated pool. They mention chlorine at a\nlevel of 30mg/L for 4 hours as being necessary to kill cryptosporidium\noocysts but make no recommendations on the use of chlorine. Further\ndetailed advice is given on the use of coagulants and filters.\n5. In the UK there is concern on relying on high chlorine levels alone as a\nmeans of disinfection because\na. There are no published data on the effect of chlorine on cryptosporidium\noocysts in a swimming pool. It is understood that the studies that have\nbeen undertaken are in a laboratory setting.\nb. Chlorine is inactivated by organic material and there may be high levels\nof such material in parts of a pool and especially in filters.\nc. It would be necessary for the chlorine to be evenly distributed\nthroughout the pool.\nd. For chlorine to be effective the pH must be carefully controlled (CDC\nrecommend 7.2 - 7.5)\ne. Other chemicals may interfere with the action of the chlorine.\n6. Concerns with filtration include\na. The need for the filters to be operated at the correct pressure and\nwater flow. The optimum operating conditions should be provided by the\nfilter suppliers\nb. The use of sand of the correct particle size in the filters.\nc. Proper maintenance and back washing of the filters.\nd. The time taken for all the pool water to be filtered. Water equivalent\nto the pool volume may pass through in a given time, but the structure of\nthe pool may be such that all the water has not passed through. That is\nthere may be areas of poor circulation in the pool.\n7. Water can be tested for the presence of cryptosporidium\noocysts. Normally 10L grab samples are tested but larger volumes can be\nexamined by the use of a cartridge filter. The volume of water and\ncollection method should be discussed with the test laboratory. Before\nconsidering the appropriate action, an understanding of the water test\nresults is necessary so that misunderstandings do not occur.\na. Cryptosporidium oocysts are not evenly distributed in the\nwater. Therefore the numbers in a sample do not on their own have any\nsignificance. If oocysts are present, that water is contaminated and poses\na potential risk to bathers.\nb. The absence of oocysts in a sample does not mean that there are no\noocysts elsewhere in the pool that could be a hazard to bathers.\nc. All laboratory tests should be undertaken in a laboratory with\naccreditation for cryptosporidium testing in water. The test method should\nbe based on the US EPA Analytical Method 1662 \"Cryptosporidium in Water by\nFiltraion/IMS/FA\"\nd. The tests undertaken do not distinguish between live and dead\noocysts. As a rule of thumb they should all be regarded as alive and\npotentially infectious.\ne. If a suspect pool has negative test results for oocysts, further samples\n-- including a backwash and a sample of sand from the top 30cm of the\nfilter -- should be examined.\nf. Examining the deposit from the pump strainer is also worth considering.\n8. The following scenarios are being met associated with hotel pools.\na. 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families using a\nswimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses with\ndiarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. Pool water\nsamples containing cryptosporidium oocysts\nb. As for (a) with no oocysts demonstrated in water samples.\nc. Oocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea.\n9. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool\nusers. Oocysts found in water tests\na. Empty the pool\nb. Thoroughly clean all pipe work and associated tanks\nc. Replace the sand in the filters\nd. Refill the pool and chlorinate to 20 mg/L at pH 7.2 - 7.5 for 8 hours\n10. Scenario 3 or more cases of cryptosporidiosis from different families\nusing a swimming pool. This may be accompanied by an increase in illnesses\nwith diarrhoea, and no other obvious cause, among other pool users. No\noocysts found in water tests\na. Test further water samples and especially backwash samples. Test\nsamples of sand taken from the top 30 cm of the sand filters. If negative\nand further cases do not occur, no further action need be taken and another\nsource of infection should be sought.\nb. Look for non-pool source(s) of infection. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. This should involve the local\npublic health authorities and the Water Company or companies.\nc. If further cases occur and the pool is the probable source of infection,\nfollow the scenario below, even if oocyst are not found in the pool water\ntests.\n11. Scenario\nOocysts demonstrated in pool water samples but no associated cases of\nunexplained diarrhoea\na. Take further samples including sand from the filters.\nb. Close the pool and any other pools whose water treatment is linked to\nthe fouled pool.\nc. Maintaining disinfectant levels at the top of the operating range,\nvacuum and sweep the pool.\nd. Using optimised coagulation, filter for 6 turnover cycles (which may\nmean closing the pool for a day). This assumes good hydraulics and filters\nthat have been correctly installed and maintained.\ne. Backwash the filters individually, NOT through other filters.\nf. Check free chlorine residuals and pH and if they are satisfactory reopen\nthe pool.\n12. General Considerations in all scenarios\na. Ensure that any water used to fill or top up the pool is free from\nCryptosporidium\nb. Ensure that the pool operation is in accordance with sound water\ntreatment principles as for example in the PWTAG Swimming Pool Water\n-Treatment and Quality Standards (1999)\nc. Consider other pools or water features that may be contaminated.\nd. Do not move guests from a pool with a contaminated pool to another\nhotel. This may spread any infection.\ne. Ensure that all test results are made available to the local public\nhealth authorities\nReferences:\nCDC Guidelines:\nPWTAG:\nSWIMMING POOL WATER - Treatment and Quality Standards (ISBN 0 951 70076 6).\nObtainable through:\nSee also Institute of Sport and Recreation Management:\n\nThese pilot guidelines have been discussed with other workers in the U.K.\nwho have experience in the management of swimming pool outbreaks, and there\nis a general agreement on the contents.\n--\nProf. Rodney Cartwright,\nMicroDiagnostics (UK) Ltd,\nGuildford, England\n<cartwright@dial.pipex.com>\n[ProMED thanks Professor Rodney Cartwright for the thorough review of the\nexisting guidelines for cleaning swimming pools and the update on the\nnumber of infected UK tourists. We have not received any reports from other\ncountries of residents at the particular hotel who have diarrhoea. - Mod.EP]\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pool - Spain (Majorca) 20030730.1869\n2002\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (03) 20020808.4995\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) (02) 20020806.4957\nCryptosporidiosis, water supply - UK (Scotland) 20020314.3743\n2001\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - Germany 20011006.2421\n2000\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, human ex sheep - UK (England) 20000419.0569\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (Scotland) 20000605.0903\n1999\n----\nCryptosporidiosis, swimming pools - UK (England) 19991125.2087\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 19990514.0788\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (NW England) 1990514201611\nCryptosporidiosis, human - New Zealand (02) 1991018082203\n1998\n----\nCryptosporidiosis - UK 19980510215948\nCryptosporidiosis - UK (04) 19980520072311\nCryptosporidiosis - Australia (Victoria) (02) 1980404084359\n....................mpp/ep/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The local water company declared a major incident on 23 April,\nwhen the results of testing became available, and met with consultants in\ncommunicable disease control (CCDCs) and regional epidemiologists the next\nday. It was agreed that a 'boil water' notice would be inappropriate given\nthat most of the water would have already been used and subsequent samples\nwere within the normal range (less than or equal to 0.2/L), but that\nsurveillance for cryptosporidiosis should be enhanced.\nDuring the following week it became clear that several health authorities\nwere reporting a sudden increase in reports of cryptosporidiosis. CDSC\nNorth West is aware of 217 cases reported to CCDCs between 1 April and 12\nMay, 188 of whom have been reported since 25 April. The occurrence of 188\ncases in 17 days in a population of 1.5 million represents a crude\nannualised incidence of 270 per 100,000 population, compared with 7/100,000\nin England and Wales in 1998 overall (based on 3745 laboratory reports and\na population of 52 million).\nFaecal specimens from affected people have been typed at the PHLS Food\nHygiene Laboratory: all have been identified as _Cryptosporidium parvum_ of\ngenotype 2 (the zoonotic strain. _Cryptosporidium_ has also been identified\nin 12 out of 32 faecal specimens taken from sheep grazing around the\nreservoir.\n[Reported by Paul Hunter, Chester Public Health Laboratory, Chester, England\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n...................................jw/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The local water company declared a major incident on 23 April,\nwhen the results of testing became available, and met with consultants in\ncommunicable disease control (CCDCs) and regional epidemiologists the next\nday. It was agreed that a 'boil water' notice would be inappropriate given\nthat most of the water would have already been used and subsequent samples\nwere within the normal range (less than or equal to 0.2/L), but that\nsurveillance for cryptosporidiosis should be enhanced.\nDuring the following week it became clear that several health authorities\nwere reporting a sudden increase in reports of cryptosporidiosis. CDSC\nNorth West is aware of 217 cases reported to CCDCs between 1 April and 12\nMay, 188 of whom have been reported since 25 April. The occurrence of 188\ncases in 17 days in a population of 1.5 million represents a crude\nannualised incidence of 270 per 100,000 population, compared with 7/100,000\nin England and Wales in 1998 overall (based on 3745 laboratory reports and\na population of 52 million).\nFaecal specimens from affected people have been typed at the PHLS Food\nHygiene Laboratory: all have been identified as _Cryptosporidium parvum_ of\ngenotype 2 (the zoonotic strain. _Cryptosporidium_ has also been identified\nin 12 out of 32 faecal specimens taken from sheep grazing around the\nreservoir.\n[Reported by Paul Hunter, Chester Public Health Laboratory, Chester, England\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n...................................jw/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\n\nAnglian Water was cited as saying that cryptosporidium had been found in a\nwater sample taken at a treatment works in Marham, Norfolk, and a boil water\nadvisory was issued for around 80,000 people.\nResidents in the nearby towns of Downham Market, Wisbech and March were\nbeing advised to take precautions until the problem was eradicated.\nA spokesman for Anglian Water was cited as saying there were no known cases\nof sickness in the area as a result of the infection, adding, \"This was\nfound in a water sample taken at our treatment works and, in conjunction\nwith local health authority officials, we are advising people in the\naffected areas to boil water as a precaution. We apologise for the\ninconvenience but the health and safety of customers must come first. Tests\nare now being carried out to find and eradicate the source of the problem.\nAt the moment we cannot say how long it will be before everything is back to\nnormal.\"\nThe scare comes 14 months after people in 300,000 homes in Hertfordshire and\nnorth London were told to boil water after Cryptosporidium was found in\nsupplies.\nAt least 30 people had stomach ailments thought to have been caused by the\ninfection.\n--\nRobert A. LaBudde, PhD, PAS, Dpl. ACAFS e-mail: ral@lcfltd.com\nLeast Cost Formulations, Ltd. URL: http://lcfltd.com/\n824 Timberlake Drive Tel: 757-467-0954\nVirginia Beach, VA 23464-3239 Fax: 757-467-2947\n***\n[2\nDate: Sun, 10 May 1998 14:00:44 -0500\nFrom: Martin Hugh-Jones <mehj2020@vt8200.vetmed.lsu.edu>\nSource: EUROSURVEILANCE, Week 19: 7 May, 1998,\n<http://www.eurosurv.org/main.htm>\n\nIncreased cases of Cryptosporidium infection in central Scotland\nAbout 120 Cryptosporidium infections have been reported to departments of\npublic health medicine in the central belt of Scotland (Argyll & Clyde,\nForth Valley, Greater Glasgow, Lanarkshire, and Lothian Health Boards) since\nthe beginning of April, many of them in week 18. Reports of\ncryptosporidiosis in Scotland usually peak in late March and April, but few\nof the recent 120 cases are included in the national total of 168 cases\nreported to the Scottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health\n(SCIEH) in the first 18 weeks of 1998. The true total to date is likely to\nbe 200-250, considerably higher than the total of 144 for the first 18 weeks\nof 1997. Between 600 and 900 cases have been reported in each of the past\neight years. Cryptosporidiosis is a protozoal infection that causes profuse\nwatery diarrhoea and abdominal pain, sometimes with anorexia, vomiting, and\nfever. Infection may be asymptomatic, but the disease can be life\nthreatening in immunocompromised patients.\nConsultants in public health medicine from central Scotland, relevant water\nauthorities, the Scottish Parasite Reference Laboratory, SCIEH, and the\nScottish Office met on 1 May to consider the situation. Most of the recent\ncases of cryptosporidiosis are children, but all age groups are represented.\nA few patients have been admitted to hospital, including two elderly women.\nA substantial proportion of the cases are thought to be due to animal\ncontact, particularly at 'petting farms' or animal markets, where children\nare encouraged to handle young animals. Cases are known to have consumed\npotable water from several different public water supplies, and routine\nwater monitoring has shown no deviation from baseline levels. Risk factors\namong individual cases are being investigated more closely. It seems likely\nthat several factors have contributed to this outbreak.\nPress statements have emphasised the need for careful hygiene when handling\nfarm animals or sick pets and to prevent spread from person to person, and\nhave advised against the consumption of untreated water from any source.\nPeople in the UK with impaired immunity (for example, patients with AIDS or\non renal dialysis) have already been advised not to drink unboiled water\nfrom any source (1), and only water that has been boiled previously should\nbe given to babies to drink or used to reconstitute their feeds. Water\nsupplies are being monitored more closely, local health professionals have\nbeen advised, and farmers and teachers are being sent information on health\nand safety at open farms.\nReference:\n1.Department of the Environment and Department of Health. Cryptosporidium in\nwater supplies. Second report of the group of experts ('Badenoch Report').\nLondon: HMSO, 1995\n--\nReported by Peter Christie\nScottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health,\nGlasgow, Scotland\ne-mail: christie@scieh.tcom.co.uk\n\n[The Editors of Eurosurveilance had commented:\n>From the editors: \"The emergence of cryptosporidiosis\"\nOne of the first issues of Eurosurveillance reported the first outbreak of\ncryptosporidiosis identified in the Netherlands (1). Awareness of the\nimportance of the coccidian protozoon Cryptosporidium parvum has increased\nsince it was first identified as a cause of human enteritis in 1976.\nCryptosporidiosis is particularly severe and protracted in people with\nimpaired immunity, but can cause several weeks of debility and watery\ndiarrhoea in immunocompetent people. Surveys of stools carried out in\ndeveloped countries have shown the prevalence of infection to be between <1%\nand 4.5%, and many infections are subclinical (2). Children under 2 years of\nage (and their carers), animal handlers, travellers, and homosexual men are\nparticularly likely to be infected. A quick look through past issues of CDR\nReview identified outbreaks associated with contaminated water supplies (3),\ncontact with animals (including by children visiting open farms) (4-6),\nswimming pools (7,8), and consumption of inadequately pasteurised milk (9).\nThis issue of Eurosurveillance Weekly reports on the recent observation of a\nhigher than expected number of cases in Scotland. It is too early to know\nthe reason(s) for this observation, but advice about personal hygiene and\nsanitary disposal of faeces, handwashing after contact with animals, and the\nadvice that drinking water for people with impaired immunity and for babies\nshould always be boiled are generally applicable.\nReferences:\n1.van Asperen IA, Mank T, Medema GJ, Stijnen C, de Boer AS, Groot JF, et al.\nAn outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in the Netherlands. Eurosurveillance 1996;\n1: 11-2. <http://www.ceses.org/eurosurv/>\n\n2.Benenson AS. Control of communicable diseases manual. Sixteenth edition.\nWashington DC: American Public Health Association, 1995.\n\n3.Morgan D, Allaby M, Crook S, Casemore D, Healing TD, Soltanpoor N, et al.\nWaterborne cryptosporidiosis associated with a borehole supply. Commun Dis\nRep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R93-7.\n\n4.Dawson A, Griffin R, Fleetwood A, Barrett NJ. Farm visits and zoonoses.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R81-6.\n\n5.Evans MR, Gardner D. Cryptosporidiosis associated with an educational farm\nholiday. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1996; 6: R50-1 (correction R67).\n\n6.Sayers GM, Dillon MC, Connolly E, Thornton L, Hyland E, Loughman E, et al.\nCryptosporidosis in children who visited an open farm. Commun Dis Rep CDR\nRev 1996; 6:R140-4.\n\n7.Hunt DA, Sebugwawo S, Edmondson SG, Casemore DP. Cryptosporidiosis\nassociated with a swimming pool complex. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1994; 4:\nR20-2 (correction R36).\n\n8.Sundkvist T, Dryden M, Gabb R, Soltanpoor N, Casemore D, Stuart J.\nOutbreak of cryptosporidiosis associated with a swimming pool in Andover.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1997; 7: R190-2.\n\n9.Djuretic T, Wall PG, Nichols G. General outbreaks of infectious intestinal\ndisease associated with milk and dairy products in England and Wales: 1992\nto 1996. Awareness of the\nimportance of the coccidian protozoon Cryptosporidium parvum has increased\nsince it was first identified as a cause of human enteritis in 1976.\nCryptosporidiosis is particularly severe and protracted in people with\nimpaired immunity, but can cause several weeks of debility and watery\ndiarrhoea in immunocompetent people. Surveys of stools carried out in\ndeveloped countries have shown the prevalence of infection to be between <1%\nand 4.5%, and many infections are subclinical (2). Children under 2 years of\nage (and their carers), animal handlers, travellers, and homosexual men are\nparticularly likely to be infected. A quick look through past issues of CDR\nReview identified outbreaks associated with contaminated water supplies (3),\ncontact with animals (including by children visiting open farms) (4-6),\nswimming pools (7,8), and consumption of inadequately pasteurised milk (9).\nThis issue of Eurosurveillance Weekly reports on the recent observation of a\nhigher than expected number of cases in Scotland. It is too early to know\nthe reason(s) for this observation, but advice about personal hygiene and\nsanitary disposal of faeces, handwashing after contact with animals, and the\nadvice that drinking water for people with impaired immunity and for babies\nshould always be boiled are generally applicable.\nReferences:\n1.van Asperen IA, Mank T, Medema GJ, Stijnen C, de Boer AS, Groot JF, et al.\nAn outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in the Netherlands. Eurosurveillance 1996;\n1: 11-2. <http://www.ceses.org/eurosurv/>\n\n2.Benenson AS. Control of communicable diseases manual. Sixteenth edition.\nWashington DC: American Public Health Association, 1995.\n\n3.Morgan D, Allaby M, Crook S, Casemore D, Healing TD, Soltanpoor N, et al.\nWaterborne cryptosporidiosis associated with a borehole supply. Commun Dis\nRep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R93-7.\n\n4.Dawson A, Griffin R, Fleetwood A, Barrett NJ. Farm visits and zoonoses.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R81-6.\n\n5.Evans MR, Gardner D. Cryptosporidiosis associated with an educational farm\nholiday. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1996; 6: R50-1 (correction R67).\n\n6.Sayers GM, Dillon MC, Connolly E, Thornton L, Hyland E, Loughman E, et al.\nCryptosporidosis in children who visited an open farm. England (South-East) 970310233335\nDate: Fri, 08 May 1998 12:47:26 -0400\nFrom: Robert A. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\n\nAnglian Water was cited as saying that cryptosporidium had been found in a\nwater sample taken at a treatment works in Marham, Norfolk, and a boil water\nadvisory was issued for around 80,000 people.\nResidents in the nearby towns of Downham Market, Wisbech and March were\nbeing advised to take precautions until the problem was eradicated.\nA spokesman for Anglian Water was cited as saying there were no known cases\nof sickness in the area as a result of the infection, adding, \"This was\nfound in a water sample taken at our treatment works and, in conjunction\nwith local health authority officials, we are advising people in the\naffected areas to boil water as a precaution. We apologise for the\ninconvenience but the health and safety of customers must come first. Tests\nare now being carried out to find and eradicate the source of the problem.\nAt the moment we cannot say how long it will be before everything is back to\nnormal.\"\nThe scare comes 14 months after people in 300,000 homes in Hertfordshire and\nnorth London were told to boil water after Cryptosporidium was found in\nsupplies.\nAt least 30 people had stomach ailments thought to have been caused by the\ninfection.\n--\nRobert A. LaBudde, PhD, PAS, Dpl. ACAFS e-mail: ral@lcfltd.com\nLeast Cost Formulations, Ltd. URL: http://lcfltd.com/\n824 Timberlake Drive Tel: 757-467-0954\nVirginia Beach, VA 23464-3239 Fax: 757-467-2947\n***\n[2\nDate: Sun, 10 May 1998 14:00:44 -0500\nFrom: Martin Hugh-Jones <mehj2020@vt8200.vetmed.lsu.edu>\nSource: EUROSURVEILANCE, Week 19: 7 May, 1998,\n<http://www.eurosurv.org/main.htm>\n\nIncreased cases of Cryptosporidium infection in central Scotland\nAbout 120 Cryptosporidium infections have been reported to departments of\npublic health medicine in the central belt of Scotland (Argyll & Clyde,\nForth Valley, Greater Glasgow, Lanarkshire, and Lothian Health Boards) since\nthe beginning of April, many of them in week 18. Reports of\ncryptosporidiosis in Scotland usually peak in late March and April, but few\nof the recent 120 cases are included in the national total of 168 cases\nreported to the Scottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health\n(SCIEH) in the first 18 weeks of 1998. The true total to date is likely to\nbe 200-250, considerably higher than the total of 144 for the first 18 weeks\nof 1997. Between 600 and 900 cases have been reported in each of the past\neight years. Cryptosporidiosis is a protozoal infection that causes profuse\nwatery diarrhoea and abdominal pain, sometimes with anorexia, vomiting, and\nfever. Infection may be asymptomatic, but the disease can be life\nthreatening in immunocompromised patients.\nConsultants in public health medicine from central Scotland, relevant water\nauthorities, the Scottish Parasite Reference Laboratory, SCIEH, and the\nScottish Office met on 1 May to consider the situation. Most of the recent\ncases of cryptosporidiosis are children, but all age groups are represented.\nA few patients have been admitted to hospital, including two elderly women.\nA substantial proportion of the cases are thought to be due to animal\ncontact, particularly at 'petting farms' or animal markets, where children\nare encouraged to handle young animals. Cases are known to have consumed\npotable water from several different public water supplies, and routine\nwater monitoring has shown no deviation from baseline levels. Risk factors\namong individual cases are being investigated more closely. It seems likely\nthat several factors have contributed to this outbreak.\nPress statements have emphasised the need for careful hygiene when handling\nfarm animals or sick pets and to prevent spread from person to person, and\nhave advised against the consumption of untreated water from any source.\nPeople in the UK with impaired immunity (for example, patients with AIDS or\non renal dialysis) have already been advised not to drink unboiled water\nfrom any source (1), and only water that has been boiled previously should\nbe given to babies to drink or used to reconstitute their feeds. Water\nsupplies are being monitored more closely, local health professionals have\nbeen advised, and farmers and teachers are being sent information on health\nand safety at open farms.\nReference:\n1.Department of the Environment and Department of Health. Cryptosporidium in\nwater supplies. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\n\nAnglian Water was cited as saying that cryptosporidium had been found in a\nwater sample taken at a treatment works in Marham, Norfolk, and a boil water\nadvisory was issued for around 80,000 people.\nResidents in the nearby towns of Downham Market, Wisbech and March were\nbeing advised to take precautions until the problem was eradicated.\nA spokesman for Anglian Water was cited as saying there were no known cases\nof sickness in the area as a result of the infection, adding, \"This was\nfound in a water sample taken at our treatment works and, in conjunction\nwith local health authority officials, we are advising people in the\naffected areas to boil water as a precaution. We apologise for the\ninconvenience but the health and safety of customers must come first. Tests\nare now being carried out to find and eradicate the source of the problem.\nAt the moment we cannot say how long it will be before everything is back to\nnormal.\"\nThe scare comes 14 months after people in 300,000 homes in Hertfordshire and\nnorth London were told to boil water after Cryptosporidium was found in\nsupplies.\nAt least 30 people had stomach ailments thought to have been caused by the\ninfection.\n--\nRobert A. LaBudde, PhD, PAS, Dpl. ACAFS e-mail: ral@lcfltd.com\nLeast Cost Formulations, Ltd. URL: http://lcfltd.com/\n824 Timberlake Drive Tel: 757-467-0954\nVirginia Beach, VA 23464-3239 Fax: 757-467-2947\n***\n[2\nDate: Sun, 10 May 1998 14:00:44 -0500\nFrom: Martin Hugh-Jones <mehj2020@vt8200.vetmed.lsu.edu>\nSource: EUROSURVEILANCE, Week 19: 7 May, 1998,\n<http://www.eurosurv.org/main.htm>\n\nIncreased cases of Cryptosporidium infection in central Scotland\nAbout 120 Cryptosporidium infections have been reported to departments of\npublic health medicine in the central belt of Scotland (Argyll & Clyde,\nForth Valley, Greater Glasgow, Lanarkshire, and Lothian Health Boards) since\nthe beginning of April, many of them in week 18. Reports of\ncryptosporidiosis in Scotland usually peak in late March and April, but few\nof the recent 120 cases are included in the national total of 168 cases\nreported to the Scottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health\n(SCIEH) in the first 18 weeks of 1998. The true total to date is likely to\nbe 200-250, considerably higher than the total of 144 for the first 18 weeks\nof 1997. Between 600 and 900 cases have been reported in each of the past\neight years. Cryptosporidiosis is a protozoal infection that causes profuse\nwatery diarrhoea and abdominal pain, sometimes with anorexia, vomiting, and\nfever. Infection may be asymptomatic, but the disease can be life\nthreatening in immunocompromised patients.\nConsultants in public health medicine from central Scotland, relevant water\nauthorities, the Scottish Parasite Reference Laboratory, SCIEH, and the\nScottish Office met on 1 May to consider the situation. Most of the recent\ncases of cryptosporidiosis are children, but all age groups are represented.\nA few patients have been admitted to hospital, including two elderly women.\nA substantial proportion of the cases are thought to be due to animal\ncontact, particularly at 'petting farms' or animal markets, where children\nare encouraged to handle young animals. Cases are known to have consumed\npotable water from several different public water supplies, and routine\nwater monitoring has shown no deviation from baseline levels. Risk factors\namong individual cases are being investigated more closely. It seems likely\nthat several factors have contributed to this outbreak.\nPress statements have emphasised the need for careful hygiene when handling\nfarm animals or sick pets and to prevent spread from person to person, and\nhave advised against the consumption of untreated water from any source.\nPeople in the UK with impaired immunity (for example, patients with AIDS or\non renal dialysis) have already been advised not to drink unboiled water\nfrom any source (1), and only water that has been boiled previously should\nbe given to babies to drink or used to reconstitute their feeds. Water\nsupplies are being monitored more closely, local health professionals have\nbeen advised, and farmers and teachers are being sent information on health\nand safety at open farms.\nReference:\n1.Department of the Environment and Department of Health. Cryptosporidium in\nwater supplies. Second report of the group of experts ('Badenoch Report').\nLondon: HMSO, 1995\n--\nReported by Peter Christie\nScottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health,\nGlasgow, Scotland\ne-mail: christie@scieh.tcom.co.uk\n\n[The Editors of Eurosurveilance had commented:\n>From the editors: \"The emergence of cryptosporidiosis\"\nOne of the first issues of Eurosurveillance reported the first outbreak of\ncryptosporidiosis identified in the Netherlands (1). Awareness of the\nimportance of the coccidian protozoon Cryptosporidium parvum has increased\nsince it was first identified as a cause of human enteritis in 1976.\nCryptosporidiosis is particularly severe and protracted in people with\nimpaired immunity, but can cause several weeks of debility and watery\ndiarrhoea in immunocompetent people. Surveys of stools carried out in\ndeveloped countries have shown the prevalence of infection to be between <1%\nand 4.5%, and many infections are subclinical (2). Children under 2 years of\nage (and their carers), animal handlers, travellers, and homosexual men are\nparticularly likely to be infected. A quick look through past issues of CDR\nReview identified outbreaks associated with contaminated water supplies (3),\ncontact with animals (including by children visiting open farms) (4-6),\nswimming pools (7,8), and consumption of inadequately pasteurised milk (9).\nThis issue of Eurosurveillance Weekly reports on the recent observation of a\nhigher than expected number of cases in Scotland. It is too early to know\nthe reason(s) for this observation, but advice about personal hygiene and\nsanitary disposal of faeces, handwashing after contact with animals, and the\nadvice that drinking water for people with impaired immunity and for babies\nshould always be boiled are generally applicable.\nReferences:\n1.van Asperen IA, Mank T, Medema GJ, Stijnen C, de Boer AS, Groot JF, et al.\nAn outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in the Netherlands. Eurosurveillance 1996;\n1: 11-2. <http://www.ceses.org/eurosurv/>\n\n2.Benenson AS. Control of communicable diseases manual. Sixteenth edition.\nWashington DC: American Public Health Association, 1995.\n\n3.Morgan D, Allaby M, Crook S, Casemore D, Healing TD, Soltanpoor N, et al.\nWaterborne cryptosporidiosis associated with a borehole supply. Commun Dis\nRep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R93-7.\n\n4.Dawson A, Griffin R, Fleetwood A, Barrett NJ. Farm visits and zoonoses.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R81-6.\n\n5.Evans MR, Gardner D. Cryptosporidiosis associated with an educational farm\nholiday. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1996; 6: R50-1 (correction R67).\n\n6.Sayers GM, Dillon MC, Connolly E, Thornton L, Hyland E, Loughman E, et al.\nCryptosporidosis in children who visited an open farm. Commun Dis Rep CDR\nRev 1996; 6:R140-4.\n\n7.Hunt DA, Sebugwawo S, Edmondson SG, Casemore DP. Cryptosporidiosis\nassociated with a swimming pool complex. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1994; 4:\nR20-2 (correction R36).\n\n8.Sundkvist T, Dryden M, Gabb R, Soltanpoor N, Casemore D, Stuart J.\nOutbreak of cryptosporidiosis associated with a swimming pool in Andover.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1997; 7: R190-2.\n\n9.Djuretic T, Wall PG, Nichols G. General outbreaks of infectious intestinal\ndisease associated with milk and dairy products in England and Wales: 1992\nto 1996. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1997; 7: R41-5.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n......................................mhj/es\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. UK", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2193346", "date": "1998-05-10 23:50:00", "main_text": "CRYPTOSPORIDIOSIS - UK\n**********************\nA ProMED-mail post\nSee Also\nCryptosporidiosis - England (South-East) 970310233335\nDate: Fri, 08 May 1998 12:47:26 -0400\nFrom: Robert A. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\n\nAnglian Water was cited as saying that cryptosporidium had been found in a\nwater sample taken at a treatment works in Marham, Norfolk, and a boil water\nadvisory was issued for around 80,000 people.\nResidents in the nearby towns of Downham Market, Wisbech and March were\nbeing advised to take precautions until the problem was eradicated.\nA spokesman for Anglian Water was cited as saying there were no known cases\nof sickness in the area as a result of the infection, adding, \"This was\nfound in a water sample taken at our treatment works and, in conjunction\nwith local health authority officials, we are advising people in the\naffected areas to boil water as a precaution. We apologise for the\ninconvenience but the health and safety of customers must come first. Tests\nare now being carried out to find and eradicate the source of the problem.\nAt the moment we cannot say how long it will be before everything is back to\nnormal.\"\nThe scare comes 14 months after people in 300,000 homes in Hertfordshire and\nnorth London were told to boil water after Cryptosporidium was found in\nsupplies.\nAt least 30 people had stomach ailments thought to have been caused by the\ninfection.\n--\nRobert A. LaBudde, PhD, PAS, Dpl. ACAFS e-mail: ral@lcfltd.com\nLeast Cost Formulations, Ltd. URL: http://lcfltd.com/\n824 Timberlake Drive Tel: 757-467-0954\nVirginia Beach, VA 23464-3239 Fax: 757-467-2947\n***\n[2\nDate: Sun, 10 May 1998 14:00:44 -0500\nFrom: Martin Hugh-Jones <mehj2020@vt8200.vetmed.lsu.edu>\nSource: EUROSURVEILANCE, Week 19: 7 May, 1998,\n<http://www.eurosurv.org/main.htm>\n\nIncreased cases of Cryptosporidium infection in central Scotland\nAbout 120 Cryptosporidium infections have been reported to departments of\npublic health medicine in the central belt of Scotland (Argyll & Clyde,\nForth Valley, Greater Glasgow, Lanarkshire, and Lothian Health Boards) since\nthe beginning of April, many of them in week 18. Reports of\ncryptosporidiosis in Scotland usually peak in late March and April, but few\nof the recent 120 cases are included in the national total of 168 cases\nreported to the Scottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health\n(SCIEH) in the first 18 weeks of 1998. The true total to date is likely to\nbe 200-250, considerably higher than the total of 144 for the first 18 weeks\nof 1997. Between 600 and 900 cases have been reported in each of the past\neight years. Cryptosporidiosis is a protozoal infection that causes profuse\nwatery diarrhoea and abdominal pain, sometimes with anorexia, vomiting, and\nfever. Infection may be asymptomatic, but the disease can be life\nthreatening in immunocompromised patients.\nConsultants in public health medicine from central Scotland, relevant water\nauthorities, the Scottish Parasite Reference Laboratory, SCIEH, and the\nScottish Office met on 1 May to consider the situation. Most of the recent\ncases of cryptosporidiosis are children, but all age groups are represented.\nA few patients have been admitted to hospital, including two elderly women.\nA substantial proportion of the cases are thought to be due to animal\ncontact, particularly at 'petting farms' or animal markets, where children\nare encouraged to handle young animals. Cases are known to have consumed\npotable water from several different public water supplies, and routine\nwater monitoring has shown no deviation from baseline levels. Risk factors\namong individual cases are being investigated more closely. It seems likely\nthat several factors have contributed to this outbreak.\nPress statements have emphasised the need for careful hygiene when handling\nfarm animals or sick pets and to prevent spread from person to person, and\nhave advised against the consumption of untreated water from any source.\nPeople in the UK with impaired immunity (for example, patients with AIDS or\non renal dialysis) have already been advised not to drink unboiled water\nfrom any source (1), and only water that has been boiled previously should\nbe given to babies to drink or used to reconstitute their feeds. Water\nsupplies are being monitored more closely, local health professionals have\nbeen advised, and farmers and teachers are being sent information on health\nand safety at open farms.\nReference:\n1.Department of the Environment and Department of Health. Cryptosporidium in\nwater supplies. Second report of the group of experts ('Badenoch Report').\nLondon: HMSO, 1995\n--\nReported by Peter Christie\nScottish Centre for Infection and Environmental Health,\nGlasgow, Scotland\ne-mail: christie@scieh.tcom.co.uk\n\n[The Editors of Eurosurveilance had commented:\n>From the editors: \"The emergence of cryptosporidiosis\"\nOne of the first issues of Eurosurveillance reported the first outbreak of\ncryptosporidiosis identified in the Netherlands (1). Awareness of the\nimportance of the coccidian protozoon Cryptosporidium parvum has increased\nsince it was first identified as a cause of human enteritis in 1976.\nCryptosporidiosis is particularly severe and protracted in people with\nimpaired immunity, but can cause several weeks of debility and watery\ndiarrhoea in immunocompetent people. Surveys of stools carried out in\ndeveloped countries have shown the prevalence of infection to be between <1%\nand 4.5%, and many infections are subclinical (2). Children under 2 years of\nage (and their carers), animal handlers, travellers, and homosexual men are\nparticularly likely to be infected. A quick look through past issues of CDR\nReview identified outbreaks associated with contaminated water supplies (3),\ncontact with animals (including by children visiting open farms) (4-6),\nswimming pools (7,8), and consumption of inadequately pasteurised milk (9).\nThis issue of Eurosurveillance Weekly reports on the recent observation of a\nhigher than expected number of cases in Scotland. It is too early to know\nthe reason(s) for this observation, but advice about personal hygiene and\nsanitary disposal of faeces, handwashing after contact with animals, and the\nadvice that drinking water for people with impaired immunity and for babies\nshould always be boiled are generally applicable.\nReferences:\n1.van Asperen IA, Mank T, Medema GJ, Stijnen C, de Boer AS, Groot JF, et al.\nAn outbreak of cryptosporidiosis in the Netherlands. Eurosurveillance 1996;\n1: 11-2. <http://www.ceses.org/eurosurv/>\n\n2.Benenson AS. Control of communicable diseases manual. Sixteenth edition.\nWashington DC: American Public Health Association, 1995.\n\n3.Morgan D, Allaby M, Crook S, Casemore D, Healing TD, Soltanpoor N, et al.\nWaterborne cryptosporidiosis associated with a borehole supply. Commun Dis\nRep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R93-7.\n\n4.Dawson A, Griffin R, Fleetwood A, Barrett NJ. Farm visits and zoonoses.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1995; 5: R81-6.\n\n5.Evans MR, Gardner D. Cryptosporidiosis associated with an educational farm\nholiday. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1996; 6: R50-1 (correction R67).\n\n6.Sayers GM, Dillon MC, Connolly E, Thornton L, Hyland E, Loughman E, et al.\nCryptosporidosis in children who visited an open farm. Commun Dis Rep CDR\nRev 1996; 6:R140-4.\n\n7.Hunt DA, Sebugwawo S, Edmondson SG, Casemore DP. Cryptosporidiosis\nassociated with a swimming pool complex. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1994; 4:\nR20-2 (correction R36).\n\n8.Sundkvist T, Dryden M, Gabb R, Soltanpoor N, Casemore D, Stuart J.\nOutbreak of cryptosporidiosis associated with a swimming pool in Andover.\nCommun Dis Rep CDR Rev 1997; 7: R190-2.\n\n9.Djuretic T, Wall PG, Nichols G. Australia (Victoria) 980401203143\nFrom: Martyn Kirk <Martyn.Kirk@dhs.vic.gov.au>\nDate: Fri, 3 Apr 98 13:33:22 +1000\n\nAn update on cryptosporidiosis in Victoria -\n\nSo far the Victorian Department of Human Services has been notified of 33\ncases of cryptosporidiosis in the last month. All cases were investigated\nfor possible common exposures. Some cases had not swum at any pools. Four\nswimming pools were implicated as a potential source of some infections.\n\nPool managers of these four pools were advised to treat swimming pool water\nby hyperchlorinating, and in one instance to drain the pool and change the\nfilter media. Some managers of other large swimming complexes have tested\npool backwash water for viable oocysts and detected them, resulting in them\nhyperchlorinating. The Department is not currently advocating testing\nswimming pool water for _Cryptosporidium_ due to the uncertainty associated\nwith the test and its high cost.\n\nThe Department has sent guidelines on prevention of cryptosporidiosis to all\nmunicipal council environmental health officers and swimming pool managers.\nThese have been modified from those produced by CDC\n(<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/dpd/pool_op.htm>). They advise pool managers to\nfollow proper sanitation guidelines, put up signs requesting people\nexperiencing diarrhoea recently not to swim, and to have a written plan for\nfaecal accidents.\n\nContrary to some of the media coverage, we are not seeing widespread illness\nthroughout the state. We have seen much larger numbers of cases in previous\nyears. It is highly unlikely that a third of all swimming pool patrons are\nafflicted with cryptospordiosis, as reported in the previous posting.\nDespite all this _Cryptosporidium_ certainly has captured public interest.\n--\nMartyn Kirk\nEpidemiologist\nDepartment of Human Services\nVictoria\nAustralia\ne-mail: Martyn.Kirk@dhs.vic.gov.au\n..........................................mhj/es\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. All cases were investigated\nfor possible common exposures. Some cases had not swum at any pools. Four\nswimming pools were implicated as a potential source of some infections.\n\nPool managers of these four pools were advised to treat swimming pool water\nby hyperchlorinating, and in one instance to drain the pool and change the\nfilter media. Some managers of other large swimming complexes have tested\npool backwash water for viable oocysts and detected them, resulting in them\nhyperchlorinating. The Department is not currently advocating testing\nswimming pool water for _Cryptosporidium_ due to the uncertainty associated\nwith the test and its high cost.\n\nThe Department has sent guidelines on prevention of cryptosporidiosis to all\nmunicipal council environmental health officers and swimming pool managers.\nThese have been modified from those produced by CDC\n(<http://www.cdc.gov/ncidod/dpd/pool_op.htm>). They advise pool managers to\nfollow proper sanitation guidelines, put up signs requesting people\nexperiencing diarrhoea recently not to swim, and to have a written plan for\nfaecal accidents.\n\nContrary to some of the media coverage, we are not seeing widespread illness\nthroughout the state. We have seen much larger numbers of cases in previous\nyears. It is highly unlikely that a third of all swimming pool patrons are\nafflicted with cryptospordiosis, as reported in the previous posting.\nDespite all this _Cryptosporidium_ certainly has captured public interest.\n--\nMartyn Kirk\nEpidemiologist\nDepartment of Human Services\nVictoria\nAustralia\ne-mail: Martyn.Kirk@dhs.vic.gov.au\n..........................................mhj/es\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The water company\nissued a 'boil water' notice dated 2 March to 300 000 properties.\nA team has been set up to manage this incident. CDSC (Anglia and Oxford)\nis coordinating the epidemiological studies.\nThree clusters reported in Bedfordshire and Hertfordshire since the\nmiddle of January 1997 have been investigated by local outbreak teams.\nNo common factors were identified among cases. The water supplies to the\nareas concerned are apparently distinct. Cryptosporidial oocysts were\nnot detected in any of the three water distribution systems supplying\nthe affected areas. The water companies rerouted supplies to two of the\naffected areas and introduced other precautionary measures. The cluster\nin Welwyn Garden City and Hatfield consisted of ten cases who became ill\nbetween 15 January and 7 February; no other primary cases were reported\nin February. The other clusters were in Bedford and in Luton.\nOn 5 March, cryptosporidium-like oocysts were detected in one sample of\ntreated water in the Luton area and a 'boil water' notice was issued as\na precaution.\nCryptosporidiosis is usually self limiting, but severely\nimmunocompromised people may develop severe and life threatening\ndisease.\nDr Lorna Willocks, regional epidemiologist for Anglia and Oxford\n(tel 01223 331865; fax 01223 330345) would welcome reports of\ncases of cryptosporidiosis that may be associated with these clusters.\n[see: Cryptosporidium FAQ - ask majordomo for: get promed cryptosporidium]\n...........................................................jw\n--\nJack Woodall, Ph.D., Director, ProMED-mail\nWadsworth Center, New York State Dept. of Health, Albany NY\njack.woodall@wadsworth.org\nhttp://www.fas.org/promed/\nhttp://www.healthnet.org/promed.html\nhttp://www.outbreak.org/cgi-unreg/dynaserve.exe/credits.html#jack\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
